‚óŹ Good ‚ÄĒ discussion only, no plan. Let me engage with the actual idea.

  The thesis is sound, and it has a name (several)

  What you're describing ‚ÄĒ effects are flat steps; nesting/recursion lives only 
  in the pure world ‚ÄĒ is the core discipline of synchronous dataflow languages
  (Lustre, Esterel, Signal) and flow-based programming, and it's also how
  hardware composes: Verilog modules aren't called, they're instantiated and 
  wired. Those languages are used precisely in the realtime/embedded niche arche
  targets, and they share arche's other constraints (statically bounded, no
  dynamic allocation). So this isn't exotic ‚ÄĒ you'd be pulling arche toward a
  well-explored corner that happens to fit its existing ethos. ECS
  system-scheduling (Bevy/Flecs) is the same shape with a data-oriented skin.

  And it lines up cleanly with the kinds you already have:

  - func = the compositional world. Pure, returns a value, nests and recurses
  freely. "Chasing stuff everywhere" is fine here because it's referentially
  transparent and always terminates back into a value.
  - proc = one flat effectful step. Straight-line. May descend into funcs (safe
  ‚ÄĒ that world always returns), but never into another proc.
  - map = the degenerate proc: a column kernel with no control flow at all (you
  already enforce this with E0046).
  - system = the only composer of effects. It sequences/schedules procs and maps
  and owns the storage that wires them.

  Effect call-depth becomes exactly 1 (system ‚Üí proc). Depth only exists in the
  functional world. That's the property you want.

  The one structural consequence

  The storage you remove from the call stack doesn't vanish ‚ÄĒ it reappears as 
  state the system owns. Today divmod(17,5)(q:,r:) works because the caller's 
  frame allocates q/r and lends pointers; the call stack is the dataflow medium.
  Ban proc‚Üíproc and the producing proc's frame can't hold a value its consumer
  needs later ‚ÄĒ so inter-proc values hoist up into a system-owned,
  compile-time-sized record (a blackboard / param pool).

  This is good for you: it's static, bounded, and visible ‚ÄĒ the whole wiring is
  declared in one place instead of implicit in a call tree. The cost is a
  naming/binding problem, and here's the subtle part: maps don't have this 
  problem because their "params" are archetype columns ‚ÄĒ the data already has a 
  home (a pool with a name). A proc's scalar params have no home. So to make
  procs schedulable like maps, you have to give their params a home. That's the
  whole game.

  What actually replaces a proc-call

  A proc-call does three jobs today. Splitting them shows where the design is
  easy vs. where it lives or dies:

  1. Pass arguments ‚ÄĒ mechanical. Becomes wiring: name-on-a-blackboard (simple,
  but global-mutable-within-the-system, the thing functional folks dislike),
  type-matching (elegant, arche-flavored, but ambiguous when two procs emit the
  same type), or explicit ports (a.out -> b.in ‚ÄĒ precise, verbose, i.e. a
  patch/dataflow graph). This one's fine whichever way you go.
  2. Conditional / data-dependent invocation ‚ÄĒ the fork. "Run B only if A said
  so, with args from A." In a flat model this control flow has to go somewhere,
  and there are only two homes:
    - Inside a proc ‚Üí but a proc that decides which proc runs next is calling
  one in spirit; you've smuggled the tree back in.
    - In the system spec ‚Üí then the system grows a control-flow language
  (guards, conditions, ordering). You didn't delete the call tree; you relocated
  it from procs up into the system. That may be exactly right (it's now
  declarative and inspectable) ‚ÄĒ but it's the central thing to decide.
  3. Recursion / dynamic traversal with effects ‚ÄĒ the genuine loss. Your own
  router (new_node recursing to build a trie) can't be a proc calling itself. It
  has to become either an explicit work-queue the system iterates, or a pure
  func that builds a value which a single proc then commits to a pool. Some
  effectful algorithms are fundamentally recursive, and this forces every one of
  them through "compute purely, commit once" ‚ÄĒ which is often a better
  structure, but not always a free one.

  Two smaller honest caveats: errors stop being stack-unwinding and become data
  + scheduled handlers (consistent, but a rewrite of how !abort propagates); and
  FFI is unavoidably nested ‚ÄĒ the moment a proc calls libc you're on a real C
  stack, so "flat" is a property of your code, not the process.

  The crux I'd put back to you

  Jobs (1) and the kind split are clean. The whole design rests on #2: where 
  does data-dependent control flow live?

  If the answer is "in the system spec," then a system isn't just an unordered
  bag of procs+maps ‚ÄĒ it's a small coordination language (sequence, guard, maybe
  a bounded loop/work-queue), and the procs underneath it are genuinely flat
  leaves. That's coherent and I think it's what you're reaching for. But it
  means the interesting design work moves out of procs and into what a system
  can express ‚ÄĒ and you'll feel constant pressure to let it express more until
  it's a call tree wearing a different hat.

  So before anything else: do you want the system to be able to say "run B only 
  after A, and only if A produced X"? Or should even that be forbidden ‚ÄĒ pure 
  source-order sequencing, and anything conditional becomes a map over data that
  may be empty? The first is FBP/ECS-with-scheduling; the second is closer to
  synchronous dataflow and is far more restrictive but far more analyzable. That
  choice determines basically everything else.

‚úĽ Saut√©ed for 2m 8s

‚ĚĮ mockup what it would look like if we removed the main proc, and just had a    
  way to register systems to run and schedule. The systems are composed of      
  procs and maps, no real logic aside from gluing binding together i think.     

‚óŹ Here's a concrete mockup. I'll show the same program before (today's main) and
  after (systems + a schedule), so the delta is the point.

  Today

  pos_x :: float;  pos_y :: float;
  vel_x :: float;  vel_y :: float;
  Particle :: arche { pos_x, pos_y, vel_x, vel_y };
  [10000]Particle(0);                    // cap 10000, 0 live

  integrate :: map (query { pos_x, pos_y, vel_x, vel_y }) {
    pos_x = pos_x + vel_x;
    pos_y = pos_y + vel_y;
  }
  dampen :: map (query { vel_x, vel_y }) {
    vel_x = vel_x * 0.98;
    vel_y = vel_y * 0.98;
  }

  load_seed :: proc(path: [64]char)(count: int) { /* extern read, fills pool */
  }
  spawn     :: proc(n: int)()               { /* insert n rows            */ }
  render    :: proc()()                     { /* draw the column          */ }

  main :: proc() {
    load_seed("seed.csv")(count:);   // proc calls proc  ‚Üź the thing we're
  banning
    spawn(count);                    // proc calls proc  ‚Üź "
    for (t := 0; t < 600; t = t + 1) {
      run integrate;
      run dampen;
      render();                      // proc calls proc  ‚Üź "
    }
  }

  The leaves (integrate, spawn, ‚Ä¶) are fine. The problem is main: it's a proc,
  and it's calling procs and holding a control-flow loop ‚ÄĒ that's the call tree
  and the "chasing" you want gone.

  After

  main disappears. The proc‚Üíproc calls in it get re-homed into systems (pure
  glue) and a top-level schedule (the registration/ordering). Leaves are
  unchanged.

  // --- data, funcs, procs, maps: identical to before, all leaves ---

  // A system is GLUE ONLY: it invokes procs/maps and threads their
  // out-args into later in-args by name. No operators, no if/for/match,
  // no direct func calls, no computing a value. Just wiring.

  boot :: system {
    load_seed("seed.csv")(count:);   // out-arg `count` enters system scope
    spawn(count);                    // ...and feeds the next step's in-arg
  }

  frame :: system {
    run integrate;                   // map
    run dampen;                      // map
    render();                        // proc
  }

  // The schedule replaces `main`: register systems + when they run.
  // `once` = startup; `loop` = per tick. The tick/loop is owned by the
  // runtime, not written as a `for` inside a proc.

  schedule {
    once boot;
    loop frame;
  }

  What changed, precisely

  - main is gone. The two things it did ‚ÄĒ wiring (count ‚Üí spawn) and control 
  flow (the for loop) ‚ÄĒ split apart. Wiring goes into boot/frame; the loop
  becomes loop in the schedule (the runtime owns iteration, exactly as a map
  already owns its per-row loop).
  - A system is "today's main body minus logic." Same step(in)(out:) / run
  syntax, same in-scope out-arg threading ‚ÄĒ but stripped to glue. The grammar of
  a system body is just: invoke a proc/map; bind its out-args; reuse those
  names (and literals) as later in-args. That's the whole language. Anything
  that smells like computation (+, a func call, if) is rejected ‚ÄĒ push it into a
  proc.
  - The blackboard is the system's scope. count lives in boot's scope, not on a
  call stack. It's the same hoisting you'd do by hand, made the system's job.
  Compile-time sized, visible in one place.
  - Effect call-depth is¬†1. schedule ‚Üí system ‚Üí proc(leaf) ‚Üí (funcs, pure). No
  proc reaches another proc.

  The two seams this mockup is quietly papering over

  1. Scalar wiring across systems. Inside boot, count threads fine because it's
  one scope. But what if frame needs a value boot produced? It's gone ‚ÄĒ boot's
  scope died. That value has to be promoted to persistent state ‚ÄĒ i.e. a column
  on some pool, or a named global resource the schedule owns. So in practice
  there are two tiers of "blackboard": transient (within one system's scope,
  like count) and persistent (lives across ticks ‚ÄĒ really just an
  archetype/pool). Worth deciding whether systems can have any transient scalars
  or whether every inter-step value must be a pool column (which would make
  systems even purer ‚ÄĒ pure run/call lists, no names at all).
  2. Conditional scheduling. schedule { loop frame; } runs frame every tick
  unconditionally. The moment you want "run render only when dirty," the
  condition has to land somewhere ‚ÄĒ a guard in the schedule (loop frame when 
  dirty;), or it dissolves into the data (render becomes a map over a pool
  that's empty when there's nothing to draw). This is the fork from before, now
  made concrete: does schedule get guards, or is all conditionality forced down 
  into emptiness-of-data?

  Want me to push on either of those ‚ÄĒ sketch what schedule looks like with
  guards/phases/parallelism, or sketch the stricter variant where systems have
  no named bindings at all (everything is a pool column)?

‚úĽ Baked for 5s

‚óŹ Agent "Web server proc-call chains" came to rest ¬∑ 1m 49s

‚óŹ All three repos mapped. Here's the thing the examples make obvious: every 
  proc‚Üíproc chain in your codebase falls into one of three buckets, and they 
  migrate very differently. Let me show each with real code.

  First, the rule that makes any of this tractable: the ban is arche-proc ‚Üí 
  arche-proc only. Extern procs (FFI) and funcs are exempt ‚ÄĒ the C stack is
  unavoidable anyway, and funcs are the pure "chasing" world you want to keep.
  So a legal leaf proc is one that bottoms out in externs + funcs, never another
  arche proc.

  Bucket 1 ‚ÄĒ Orchestration: clean win

  Your ETL (task_3_bucket_timestamps, csv_load) is the canonical
  read‚Üíparse‚Üícompute‚Üíwrite. Today:

  main :: proc() {
    load_transactions();                                   // proc‚Üíproc
  (csv.load)
    Transaction.price_bucket = Transaction.price / 10.0;   // already a column
  op
    write_transactions();                                  // proc‚Üíproc (io.*)
  }

  The compute step is already columnar ‚Üí it's a map. load/write become flat leaf
  procs (externs + funcs only). main dissolves into the schedule:

  bucket :: map (query { price, price_bucket }) {
    price_bucket = price / 10.0;
  }

  load_tx  :: proc()() { /* arche_file_map + find_byte/atof (funcs) ‚ÄĒ no arche
  procs */ }
  write_tx :: proc()() { /* arche_fopen + sprintf (func) + arche_fwrite */ }

  schedule {
    once load_tx;     // read + parse ‚Üí fills Transaction
    once bucket;      // compute (map: vectorizes / cores backend)
    once write_tx;    // serialize + write
  }

  This is the case you already imagined, and most application-level mains are
  this. The RPG main loop is the same shape with once‚Üíloop and run game.step
  already a map.

  Bucket 2 ‚ÄĒ Thin wrappers: forced to collapse (the real cost)

  Your stdlib is a tower of proc-over-proc-over-extern. read ‚Üí fread ‚Üí 
  os.syscall, open ‚Üí fopen_read ‚Üí syscall, respond_text ‚Üí respond, csv.load ‚Üí 
  io.file_map, new_node ‚Üí insert. Every one of those middle arche-proc layers is
  now illegal. They collapse:

  // today: read wraps fread wraps syscall  (arche-proc ‚Üí arche-proc ‚Üí extern)
  read  :: proc(f, buf)(out)    { fread(f, buf, buf.length)(r:); out = r; }
  fread :: proc(f, buf, n)(out) { os.syscall(0, f, buf, n, 0,0,0)(rr:); ... }

  // after: one flat proc, extern is the floor (allowed)
  read :: proc(f: fd, buf: []char)(out: []char) {
    os.syscall(0, f, buf, buf.length, 0,0,0)(rr:);
    out = buf[0: clamp_lo(i32(rr))];   // clamp_lo is a pure func ‚ÄĒ fine
  }

  fread either vanishes or gets duplicated where someone needs the byte count
  separately. That's the honest price: you lose the DRY layering (respond_text
  reusing respond, open reusing fopen_read). The payoff is every public proc is
  now a self-contained flat unit ‚ÄĒ no surprise about what it reaches. For your
  thin wrappers this is mechanical. new_node (just insert + an i32 cast) inlines
  into add with zero drama.

  Bucket 3 ‚ÄĒ Conditional dispatch: the actual design work

  The web request lifecycle is the hard one ‚ÄĒ dispatch in router_route_resolve
  and respond in http.arche:

  match h {
    handler.home      : respond(conn, hdr, 200, "text/html", home_html, true);
    handler.user      : respond(conn, hdr, 200, "text/html", user_html, true);
    handler.not_found : respond_text(conn, hdr, 404, "Not Found\n");
  }

  Each arm calls a (different) proc with (different) args. That's a call tree
  chosen by data ‚ÄĒ exactly what the ban forbids inside a proc, and it's logic,
  so it can't go in the glue either. The resolution is a single trick: make the 
  branch choose data, not a procedure. Collapse the N response procs into one
  uniform leaf:

  // branch selects DATA only ‚ÄĒ no proc calls
  status := 200;  ctype := "text/html";  body := home_html;
  body = select(h == handler.user, user_html, body);
  ... // not_found sets status=404, body="Not Found\n", etc.

  emit_response(conn, status, ctype, body);  // ONE flat leaf: sprintf(func) +
  net_send(extern)

  And here's the encouraging part: arche's router already cut this seam. resolve
  returns a handler_id ‚ÄĒ it deliberately does not call the handler. You're
  already halfway to data-oriented dispatch; the ban just finishes the job by
  making the handler bodies data-driven too.

  The payoff this unlocks for the server

  Once dispatch is data, the whole per-request lifecycle stops being a deep
  stack and becomes a staged pipeline over a connection pool ‚ÄĒ which is how
  high-throughput servers are actually built:

  fd :: int;  stage :: int;  nread :: int;  handler_id :: int;  status :: int;
  inbuf :: [1024]char;  outbuf :: [1024]char;     // per-conn buffer columns
  Conn :: arche { fd, stage, inbuf, nread, handler_id, status, outbuf };
  [256]Conn(0);                                    // 256 live conns, static, no
  heap

  accept_new :: proc()() { /* net_accept (extern) ‚Üí insert Conn rows  */ }
  recv_step  :: proc()() { /* per-row net_recv into inbuf, set nread   */ }
  parse_step :: proc()() { /* fill method/target columns from inbuf    */ }
  route_step :: proc()() { /* resolve ‚Üí write handler_id column         */ }
  serve_step :: proc()() { /* handler_id ‚Üí fill status/outbuf (data-dispatch) */
  }
  send_step  :: proc()() { /* net_send per row; delete completed rows   */ }

  schedule {
    loop { accept_new; recv_step; parse_step; route_step; serve_step; send_step;
  }
  }

  The deep per-request call stack became a wide per-connection data pipeline.
  Every transient that lived on the stack (method, target, handler_id, response
  bytes) is now a Conn column. And stages over disjoint columns are exactly what
  your cores backend can parallelize. This is the arche-native shape, and it's
  genuinely better than the call-stack version for a server.

  The honest residue

  What actually gets worse, so we're not selling magic:

  - DRY dies in the wrapper layer. Reuse-by-calling (respond_text‚Üírespond)
  becomes inline-or-duplicate.
  - Transient state needs a home. Stack locals become pool columns or
  system-scope bindings ‚ÄĒ more upfront data modeling. (And per-row buffer
  columns like inbuf: [1024]char are an open question for your data model ‚ÄĒ do
  archetypes carry fixed buffers cleanly today?)
  - Heterogeneous arms hurt. The match-to-data trick is free when arms differ
  only in bytes; when they differ in effects (one writes a file, one opens a
  socket), you need a stage per kind or one fat inlined proc.
  - N=1 ceremony. A CLI tool or a single connection is inherently a straight
  sequence. Forcing it through a pool-of-one is overkill; leaving it as one long
  flat proc is legal but a big proc.

  So the question your examples actually surface: for the inherently-sequential 
  single lifecycle (one CLI run, one connection), do you want it to stay one 
  long flat proc, or do you force even that through the pool-pipeline reframe?
  The pipeline is gorgeous at N‚Čę1 and pure ceremony at N=1 ‚ÄĒ and where that
  cutoff lives, and whether the language picks or you pick, decides how heavy
  this model feels in practice. Want to dig into that, or into the buffer-column
  data-modeling problem (which I think is the real blocker for the server
  pipeline)?

‚úĽ Brewed for 4m 34s

‚ĚĮ so maybe allow procs like the compiler can inline at comp time? or is that    
  confusing?                                                                    

‚óŹ Good instinct ‚ÄĒ and the honest answer is: it cleanly solves one of your two
  objections and not the other.

  You had two complaints bundled together. Pragmatic: procs grow the runtime
  stack (bad for no-heap/realtime ‚ÄĒ depth isn't statically bounded). Conceptual:
  "procedural should mean a flat list of steps, not chasing stuff everywhere."
  Compile-time inlining fully fixes the pragmatic one ‚ÄĒ if every proc‚Üíproc call
  is forced to inline, the runtime stack is flat and statically sized, exactly
  the property you want. But it does nothing for the conceptual one: the source
  still has the tree. You still chase respond_text ‚Üí respond ‚Üí 
  reason/sprintf/send when reading the code. Inlining is invisible at the source
  level, so if "chasing" is the thing that bothers you, this doesn't remove it.

  So whether it's the right call depends on which objection you actually care
  about. You said "it's pragmatic" ‚ÄĒ which points at inlining being legitimate.
  But adopting it quietly forces three sub-decisions you should make on purpose,
  not stumble into:

  1. You must ban (or compile-time-bound) recursion. "Always inline" + recursion
  = infinite expansion. So either recursion is illegal, or it needs a static
  unroll count. This is actually consistent with arche (no dynamic allocation
  already implies no unbounded recursion), and your router is already iterative
  ‚ÄĒ but it's a real language rule people will hit.
  2. Code size grows instead of stack. Every call site duplicates the body.
  respond used by 5 handlers = 5 copies; the io/csv/net wrapper towers fully
  expand at every use. You're trading stack depth for binary size ‚ÄĒ usually fine
  for this target, but it's the cost, and it's combinatorial with deep towers.
  3. No runtime proc-values. A proc whose target comes from data (a handler
  table indexed by handler_id) can't be inlined ‚ÄĒ the compiler doesn't know
  which one. So "always inline" forbids dynamic dispatch tables. Your router
  dodges this already (resolve returns an id, then a match), and arche only
  monomorphizes callbacks "in hot mode," so there's precedent ‚ÄĒ but it caps
  expressiveness.

  Is it confusing? Yes, but it's a naming problem, not a semantics one

  The semantics are clean and well-precedented (C static inline, Rust
  #[inline(always)]). What's confusing is that you'd have a proc that looks like
  a normal callable but is secretly a non-recursive, non-addressable,
  code-duplicating macro. That's "a function with holes" ‚ÄĒ and people trip on
  the holes, not the inlining.

  Two
  ‚éŅ ¬†Interrupted ¬∑ What should Claude do instead?

‚ÄĽ recap: Exploring a language-design idea for arche: ban proc-to-proc calls, compose flat systems of procs and maps under a scheduler. We've mapped real migration examples across three repos. Next: decide single-lifecycle handling or the per-row buffer-column data-modeling problem.

‚ĚĮ can't we "pass effects" between procs and have it similar to calling a proc   
  in a proc.                                                                    

‚óŹ Yes ‚ÄĒ and you've just reinvented algebraic effects / capability passing, which
  is probably the right answer to your conceptual complaint. And you already
  have the seed of it: arche's proc-typed params with callback monomorphization
  (f :: proc()(), f()() resolved to a direct call in hot mode) are exactly "pass
  a proc as an effect." This would be generalizing that into a named effect.

  The core idea: a proc doesn't call respond ‚ÄĒ it performs a reply effect. It
  never names a concrete callee. The composer installs the handler.

  effect Http { reply(status: int, ctype: []char, body: []char); }

  handle_home :: proc(path: []char) uses Http {
    reply(200, "text/html", home_html);   // reads like a call; it's a perform ‚ÄĒ
  no concrete callee
  }

  serve :: system {
    with Http = http_send(conn) {   // composer binds the effect to a real
  handler
      ...
    }
  }

  perform reply(...) reads exactly like respond(...) ‚ÄĒ so you get the
  proc-in-proc ergonomics you miss. That's the answer to your question
  literally: yes, you can pass effects and it feels like calling.

  But here's the split you have to be honest about, because it's the whole crux:

  It fixes your conceptual complaint cleanly. The proc body stays a flat list of
  performs. It depends on an effect signature, not a concrete proc, so there's
  no chasing ‚ÄĒ you read handle_home top to bottom and the wiring lives
  elsewhere, in the composer. That's the exact inversion you want: "procedural =
  flat steps; the graph is declared by whoever composes them."

  It does not fix your pragmatic complaint by itself. A performed effect is
  still a call frame at runtime. Full effect handlers need continuations
  (suspend/resume) ‚ÄĒ heap, stack capture ‚ÄĒ which is the opposite of arche's
  no-heap/flat-stack ethos. The only subset you can afford (one-shot,
  tail-resumptive: handler runs, returns, control continues) compiles to... a
  proc call. So the runtime stack still grows per perform unless you also 
  resolve-and-inline.

  Which is why effects and your inline idea aren't competitors ‚ÄĒ they're the
  same answer from two ends, and together they're the sweet spot:

  - Effects give you the principled hole (the proc declares "I perform Http",
  doesn't name who).
  - Compile-time resolution + inlining fills the hole: the composer's binding is
  known statically, so perform reply(...) monomorphizes and inlines to the real
  send.

  Get all three at once: inline ergonomics, the conceptual inversion (no chasing
  concrete procs), and a flat static stack. Effects are what make the inlining
  principled instead of ad-hoc ‚ÄĒ and crucially, effects give back the DRY the 
  outright ban took away. Under the ban, respond_text reusing respond meant
  inline-or-duplicate. Under effects, reply has one handler body that many procs
  perform ‚ÄĒ reuse without any proc naming a callee.

  The cost is real though: this is a trait/effect-row system. Signatures grow
  uses {Http, Clock}; the composer must supply a handler for every effect a proc
  performs; and static resolution re-imposes the same caps as inlining (no
  recursion through effects, no runtime dispatch tables).

  So the one decision that determines everything: statically resolved or 
  dynamically scoped?
  - Statically resolved (composer binds at compile time, inlined) ‚Üí flat stack,
  your pragmatic win, but no recursion-through-effects and no data-driven
  handler tables.
  - Dynamically scoped (real installed handlers, looked up at runtime) ‚Üí
  recursion and rich composition come back, but so do runtime frames ‚ÄĒ and the
  "chasing" returns in a new form: you now have to know which handler is 
  installed to understand what reply does.

  The first is arche-native and gives you what you asked for. The second is more
  powerful and quietly rebuilds the thing you're trying to escape. Which one
  matches your intent ‚ÄĒ flat-and-bounded, or composable-and-dynamic?

‚úĽ Cooked for 1m 22s

‚ĚĮ in that example it seems like you could use functions. And... monads? is that 
  what they are called. I need a refresher                                      

‚óŹ Good instinct on both counts ‚ÄĒ they're the same realization arriving twice.
  Quick honest refresher, then I'll tie it straight back to arche.

  What a monad actually is

  Strip the mystique: a monad is a pattern for sequencing steps where each 
  step's result feeds the next, while some "extra context" gets threaded along 
  automatically. It's two operations on a type M:

  - return : a -> M a ‚ÄĒ wrap a plain value into the context.
  - bind : M a -> (a -> M b) -> M b ‚ÄĒ "do this step, then with its result do the
  next step," handling the context plumbing in between.

  (Plus three laws so chaining is associative and return is a no-op ‚ÄĒ that's it.
  do-notation is just sugar for a chain of binds.)

  The whole zoo is just what the "extra context" is:

  ‚ĒĆ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēź
  ‚Ēā     Monad     ‚Ēā      Threads‚Ä¶      ‚Ēā      arche already has it as‚Ä¶      ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā               ‚Ēā an error /         ‚Ēā out-param ok flags + the           ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā Result/Either ‚Ēā short-circuit      ‚Ēā !abort/!clamp policy system ‚ÄĒ      ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā               ‚Ēā                    ‚Ēā (slot:, ok) is Either              ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā State s       ‚Ēā a mutable value,   ‚Ēā your blackboard / pool columns     ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā               ‚Ēā invisibly          ‚Ēā                                    ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā Reader r      ‚Ēā a read-only        ‚Ēā a config passed in / a static you  ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā               ‚Ēā environment        ‚Ēā read                               ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā Writer w      ‚Ēā an accumulated log ‚Ēā a reduce over a column             ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēā               ‚Ēā  (a monoid)        ‚Ēā                                    ‚Ēā
  ‚Ēú‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēľ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē§
  ‚Ēā IO            ‚Ēā "the real world"   ‚Ēā a proc + the schedule              ‚Ēā
  ‚ĒĒ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēī‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēė

  That table is the actual answer to your question: arche has been hand-rolling 
  the useful monads without naming them, because the no-heap rule forces you to
  unroll the abstraction into concrete machinery ‚ÄĒ out-params, policies,
  columns, the schedule.

  "You could use functions" ‚ÄĒ yes, that's the IO-monad insight

  The deepest version of the pattern (IO, and its honest cousin the Free monad)
  is: a step doesn't do an effect ‚ÄĒ it returns a value describing the effect. 
  The runtime executes the description at the edge. reply(200, "text/html", 
  body) becomes a pure func returning a Response value; a single effectful step
  at the boundary sends it.

  That's exactly the "branch chooses data, not a proc" trick from earlier, and
  exactly your proc/func split: keep the middle pure (funcs returning values), 
  push the world-touching to one effect at the edge. A proc is an IO (); the
  schedule is the interpreter that runs it. arche's architecture is already a
  heap-free encoding of the Free monad.

  Monads vs. effects (your "...or monads?")

  Same problem ‚ÄĒ structured effects in a pure world ‚ÄĒ two encodings:

  - Monads bake the context into every return type (IO a, State s a). Combining
  different effects (IO and state and errors, like any web handler) needs monad 
  transformers ‚ÄĒ stacking StateT (ReaderT IO) ‚ÄĒ which is famously clunky.
  - Algebraic effects let a proc just perform operations; the effect set is a
  row/annotation; handlers interpret. Effects combine by union, not stacking.
  This is the modern successor ‚ÄĒ "monads you don't have to manually stack."
  (Koka, OCaml 5, Unison abilities.)

  So "pass effects between procs" = algebraic effects; "monads" = the older,
  type-encoded form of the identical idea. The handler in effects is the
  interpreter in the Free monad is your schedule.

  The arche verdict

  General monads fit arche badly. bind takes a function a -> M b, and
  do-notation desugars to nested closures ‚ÄĒ they lean on higher-kinded types and
  heap closures, exactly the dynamic machinery you don't have. Importing
  Haskell's IO/transformer stack would fight the language.

  But you don't need general monads ‚ÄĒ you need the three specific ones, and you 
  already have them, just unabstracted: Result (out-param ok + policies), State
  (columns/blackboard), IO (proc + schedule). The real choice is how much to
  name that:

  - Leave it concrete (today): no abstraction, verbose, but flat and heap-free.
  - Name it as statically-resolved algebraic effects: a thin abstraction over
  what you already do ‚ÄĒ a proc declares uses {Http, Fail}, the composer supplies
  handlers, compiler inlines. You get the perform reply(...) ergonomics and the
  DRY back, without monad transformers, HKT, or closures.

  That middle option is the sweet spot we kept circling: it's "pass effects
  between procs," it reads like calling a proc, and because it resolves at
  compile time it stays flat and bounded. Monads are the same idea wearing a
  type-system coat that arche can't really afford to put on.

  Want me to sketch what a minimal, statically-resolved effect annotation would
  look like on the http example ‚ÄĒ concretely, what uses {...} + a with-handler
  buys you over the plain out-param ok you have today?

‚úĽ Crunched for 1m 15s

‚ĚĮ good analysis.                                                                
                                                                                
  so should we allow this ocaml 5 style algebraic effects in funcs?             

‚óŹ Short answer: no ‚ÄĒ not OCaml 5 style, not in funcs. And the reason is
  specific, not dogmatic: OCaml 5's two defining features are exactly the two
  things that would detonate what a func is.

  OCaml 5 effects are (a) untracked ‚ÄĒ nothing in the signature says a function
  performs them ‚ÄĒ and (b) backed by first-class one-shot continuations ‚ÄĒ perform
  captures a slice of stack and a handler resumes it. Those aren't incidental;
  they're the whole design. Drop them into arche funcs and:

  1. Purity becomes invisible and gone. Today a func's purity is a hard,
  enforced, visible rule. Untracked effects mean f(x) can do IO or mutate
  depending on which handler is installed ‚ÄĒ referential transparency dies ‚ÄĒ and 
  you can't see it in the type. That's strictly worse than your current
  proc/func split, which at least makes impurity visible at the call shape.
  2. Continuations are heap/unbounded-stack capture. Reifying a continuation to
  resume later is precisely the dynamic, non-statically-bounded allocation arche
  bans at its core. This alone disqualifies the OCaml 5 mechanism regardless of
  where you put it.
  3. The GPU/SIMD promise dies ‚ÄĒ this is the decisive one. Your maps are built
  from funcs because a func is a loopless, branch-free, per-element kernel that
  vectorizes and lowers to a compute shader. A func that can perform means a
  single lane might suspend to a handler and resume. You cannot suspend one SIMD
  lane or one warp thread to run an effect handler and resume it. The moment
  funcs can perform continuation-based effects, no func is GPU-safe, and the map
  story collapses.

  Worth noting why OCaml made effects untracked: backward compatibility with a
  giant existing codebase. arche has no such debt and already has a hard purity
  rule ‚ÄĒ so adopting the untracked part imports OCaml's compromise without
  OCaml's reason.

  But you already ship the one effect that is func-safe

  Here's the reframe. The question isn't "effects in funcs: yes/no." It's "which
  effects preserve the three properties funcs are sold on?" An effect is
  func-safe iff its handler is pure (no world mutation), tail-resumptive
  (resumes at most once, immediately, with a value ‚ÄĒ no reified continuation),
  and statically resolved (so it inlines, stays flat, vectorizes).

  That profile has a name in your codebase: the policy system. A func defaulting
  to !clamp/!zero on a fallible op is exactly a pure, continuation-free,
  compile-time-resolved effect ‚ÄĒ "Fail, handled by returning a safe value." You
  already allow one algebraic effect in funcs; you just resolve it at the op
  site instead of via a dynamic handler.

  So the coherent move, if you want more effects in funcs, is to generalize the 
  policy system ‚ÄĒ tracked in the signature, pure handlers only, tail-resumptive
  only, statically bound. That's Fail and Reader (implicit immutable env), maybe
  a Writer that's really a reduce. It is explicitly Koka-with-no-continuations,
  resolved at compile time ‚ÄĒ not OCaml 5.

  The line I'd draw

  - funcs = the pure world. Allowed effects: tracked, total, continuation-free,
  compile-time-resolved (i.e., your policies, generalized). Nothing that touches
  the world, nothing that suspends.
  - procs = the world. This is where unrestricted (statically-resolved) effects
  belong ‚ÄĒ IO, mutation, the schedule. We covered that.

  Letting funcs perform real effects collapses the two-world split you
  articulated yourself ("functional = composition; procedural = flat effectful
  steps") back into one world ‚ÄĒ and you lose the thesis, the GPU path, and the
  static stack bound all at once. Keep funcs pure; put the OCaml-5-flavored
  power in procs, and even there keep it static.

  The thing to actually decide, then, isn't funcs-vs-procs ‚ÄĒ it's whether you
  track effects (Koka-style rows, so the compiler can keep funcs honest and
  reject effectful funcs from map bodies) or leave them untracked (OCaml 5). For
  a language with a hard purity rule and a GPU target, tracking is what makes
  the whole scheme enforceable. Want to think through what a minimal effect-row
  annotation would cost you in practice ‚ÄĒ how heavy uses {...} gets before it
  becomes noise?

‚úĽ Churned for 1m 6s

‚ĚĮ okay I guess it don't understand what effects are.                            
                                                                                
  show me an example. Also use procs properly , because they don't return, they 
  write to out params.                                                          

‚óŹ Fair ‚ÄĒ let me drop the theory and show it. I'll build from a problem you
  actually have in the repo: threading an output destination down through 
  layers. I'll use procs correctly (out-params, no returns).

  The problem (real arche today)

  A proc deep in a pipeline wants to emit a line, but it must be handed
  somewhere to write it ‚ÄĒ so you thread an fd through every layer, even layers
  that don't use it themselves:

  render_row :: proc(row: int, sink: fd)() {
    buf: [64]char;
    fmt.sprintf(_, "row %d\n", row)(buf, n:);
    io.fwrite(sink, move buf, n);          // hard-committed to "write to this
  fd"
  }

  render_all :: proc(sink: fd)() {          // doesn't use sink itself...
    for (i := 0; i < 10; i = i + 1) {
      render_row(i, sink)();                // ...but must forward it (and this
  is proc‚Üíproc)
    }
  }

  Two pains: sink is plumbing noise in render_all, and render_row is welded to
  io.fwrite ‚ÄĒ to capture its output in a test you'd have to edit its body.

  The same thing with an effect

  An effect is a set of operations declared like proc signatures, with no body ‚ÄĒ
  the body is supplied later by a handler:

  effect Out {
    emit :: op(line: []char)();       // an operation: takes a line, writes no
  out-param
  }

  Now the rendering code performs emit instead of writing a sink. It takes no fd
  and forwards nothing:

  render_row :: proc(row: int)() uses Out {
    buf: [64]char;
    fmt.sprintf(_, "row %d\n", row)(buf, n:);
    emit(buf[0: n]);                  // "emit this line" ‚ÄĒ no idea where it
  goes
  }

  render_all :: proc()() uses Out {
    for (i := 0; i < 10; i = i + 1) {
      render_row(i)();                // no sink threaded; still performs Out
    }
  }

  The handler decides what emit means

  A handler interprets the effect's operations. You install it for a dynamic
  extent with with:

  // handler #1 ‚ÄĒ send emitted lines to a file
  to_file :: handler Out(sink: fd) {
    emit(line): { io.fwrite(sink, line, line.length); resume; }
  }

  write_report :: proc()() {
    io.fopen_write("out.txt")(fd:);
    with to_file(fd) {
      render_all();        // every `emit` performed in here runs to_file's body
  ‚Üí goes to fd
    }
    io.fclose(move fd);
  }

  Same render_all, different handler, different behavior ‚ÄĒ capture into a buffer
  instead of touching the disk (this is how you'd test it):

  to_buffer :: handler Out(buf: []char)(total: int) {
    emit(line): { /* copy line into buf at total; total += line.length */
  resume; }
  }

  test_report :: proc()(captured: [4096]char, total: int) {
    with to_buffer(captured)(total:) {
      render_all();        // identical render code; output now lands in
  `captured`
    }
  }

  The three moving parts

  1. perform ‚ÄĒ emit(...): "carry out this operation." Control jumps out to the
  nearest installed handler.
  2. handler ‚ÄĒ with to_file(fd) { ... }: catches every emit performed inside the
  block and runs its body.
  3. resume ‚ÄĒ at the end of the handler body, hand control back to the spot
  right after the emit, so render_row keeps going. Because the handler always
  resumes at its tail (tail-resumptive), this compiles to "call handler, return,
  continue" ‚ÄĒ no captured stack, no heap. That restriction is what keeps it
  arche-legal.

  With an out-param (honoring how procs work)

  Operations follow the proc convention ‚ÄĒ they can have an out-list, and the
  handler writes it before resume:

  effect Config {
    get_limit :: op()(limit: int);    // performs nothing in; writes `limit` out
  }

  clamp_row :: proc(row: int)(out: int) uses Config {
    get_limit()(lim:);                // perform ‚ÄĒ handler fills the out-slot
  `lim`
    out = select(row > lim, lim, row);
  }

  fixed_limit :: handler Config(n: int) {
    get_limit()(limit): { limit = n; resume; }   // write the op's out-param,
  then resume
  }

  run_clamp :: proc()(r: int) {
    with fixed_limit(100) {
      clamp_row(5)(r:);               // r = 5
    }
  }

  The op declares ()(limit: int); the perform site supplies an out-slot
  get_limit()(lim:); the handler writes that slot. No returns anywhere ‚ÄĒ exactly
  your proc model.

  The one-line definition, now that you've seen it

  An effect is a callback you didn't have to pass. A normal proc call names the
  callee and needs its dependencies (the fd) in scope and threaded down. A
  perform names only the operation; the handler is found by who's dynamically
  enclosing, and its scope holds the fd. So the plumbing (sink through
  render_all) disappears, and the same code means different things under
  different handlers.

  And tie it back to everything before: this is exactly where the proc-call tree
  went. render_all no longer chases a concrete writer ‚ÄĒ it performs Out, flat.
  The "do A then B with this sink" wiring now lives in the handler the composer
  installs. That's "pass effects between procs," made concrete.

‚úĽ Brewed for 1m 34s

‚ÄĽ recap: Exploring a language-design idea for arche: ban proc-to-proc calls so procedures stay flat, composing them via systems/schedules plus algebraic effects. We just walked through a concrete effects example; next is deciding whether to track effects in signatures and how heavy that annotation gets.

‚ĚĮ I think this might be a really good fit. Lets go back to the syntax. Should   
  we assume every proc has effects to handle? does writing handle code cause a  
  lot of nesting because nesting tends to make procedural code much harder to   
  read                                                                          

‚óŹ Both questions cut to the core, and they have a shared answer that falls out
  of a decision you've already made.

  Q1: assume every proc is effectful? ‚ÄĒ Don't annotate, infer.

  You don't need uses {...} on every proc, and here's why it's cheap in your
  design specifically: because procs can't call procs, a proc's effect set is 
  whatever it textually performs ‚ÄĒ and nothing more.

  In Koka/OCaml, effect tracking is annoying because effects propagate up the 
  call graph: if f calls g calls h and h performs E, then f's signature inherits
  E. That transitive fixpoint is the annotation tax. But you banned proc‚Üíproc
  calls. A proc performs effects only in its own body (funcs are pure, externs
  are opaque). So the effect set is local, shallow, and trivially computable ‚ÄĒ
  no fixpoint, no propagation.

  That means:

  - func = effect set must be empty (this is just your existing purity rule, now
  expressed in the same system ‚ÄĒ and it's what keeps funcs GPU-safe inside
  maps).
  - proc = performs whatever it performs; the compiler infers the set and
  displays it. You don't hand-write it.
  - A proc that performs nothing (computes out-params, maybe with !clamp
  policies) has an empty effect set ‚ÄĒ perfectly normal. So no, not every proc 
  has effects. Many leaves perform none.
  - uses {Net, Fail} stays available as an optional checked contract for public
  stdlib signatures ‚ÄĒ documentation you can opt into, not boilerplate you're
  forced to write.

  render_row :: proc(row: int)() {     // no `uses` written
    fmt.sprintf(_, "row %d\n", row)(buf, n:);
    emit(buf[0: n]);                   // compiler infers: performs {Out}
  }
  // tooling renders:  render_row :: proc(row: int)() performs {Out}

  Checking happens at the install boundary, not per-proc: if the enclosing scope
  doesn't handle Out, you get a compile error ‚ÄĒ "Out performed but no handler
  in scope." Same machinery gates funcs ("Out performed in a func ‚ÄĒ funcs must
  be pure").

  Q2: nesting ‚ÄĒ you're right, and the fix is "procs never install handlers"

  Naive effect syntax absolutely produces the pyramid you're worried about, and
  for a language whose thesis is flat, readable procedural code it would be
  fatal:

  write_report :: proc()() {
    with to_file(fd) {
      with fixed_limit(100) {
        with system_clock() {
          render_all();          // the actual work, buried three deep
        }
      }
    }
  }

  The fix is a rule, not a formatting trick: no single construct both performs 
  and installs.

  - A proc only performs ‚ÄĒ its body is a flat list of steps, never a with block.
  - A system (the composer you already designed) is the only place handlers
  install ‚ÄĒ and they install as a flat preamble, not nested blocks.

  // a handler owns its resource's whole lifecycle ‚ÄĒ no fd plumbing anywhere
  to_file :: handler Out(path: []char) {
    setup       { io.fopen_write(path)(fd:); }
    emit(line)  { io.fwrite(fd, line, line.length); resume; }
    teardown    { io.fclose(move fd); }
  }

  report :: system {
    // preamble: a FLAT list of handlers ‚ÄĒ the runtime composes the stack for
  you
    handle Out    with to_file("out.txt");
    handle Config with fixed_limit(100);
    handle Clock  with system_clock();

    // body: flat steps. every `emit`/`get_limit`/`now` performed anywhere
    // inside these procs resolves to the handlers above.
    render_all();
    flush();
  }

  The handler "stack" still exists ‚ÄĒ but you write it as a flat declaration
  list, and the runtime nests it. Three handlers, zero indentation. Proc bodies
  stay dead flat because they never install anything.

  For the occasional one-off where one step needs a different handler, a
  per-statement modifier beats reopening a block:

  report :: system {
    handle Out with to_file("out.txt");
    render_all();                          // ‚Üí file
    render_summary() with to_buffer(cap);  // just this step ‚Üí buffer
  }

  Why this all clicks

  The two big decisions reinforce each other instead of fighting:

  - Banning proc‚Üíproc makes effect sets local ‚Üí inference is trivial ‚Üí no uses
  boilerplate.
  - Having a system composer gives handlers a natural home ‚Üí installation is a
  flat preamble ‚Üí the nesting you fear never reaches proc bodies.

  So the layering is clean and each layer has exactly one job: func computes
  (pure), proc performs (flat), system installs + sequences (flat preamble +
  flat steps), schedule runs systems. Effects flow up to the system that handles
  them; nobody nests.

  The one thing left genuinely open: handler precedence and the setup/teardown 
  ordering when several handlers install in one preamble (and when one handler
  performs another's effect). That's where the real semantics live ‚ÄĒ want to
  work through how the runtime orders a flat handler list, and what happens at
  teardown if a step aborts?

‚úĽ Crunched for 1m 31s

‚ĚĮ quick side tangent. How are funcs gpu safe, because they have branches        

‚óŹ Good catch ‚ÄĒ that's a real imprecision in how I framed it. Let me correct it.

  The construct arche actually guarantees onto a GPU is the map, not the func ‚ÄĒ
  and a map is branch-free by rule: only col = expr, with select(cond, a, b) as
  the only conditional (E0046 rejects if/for/match). So "the GPU-safe thing" is
  the map, and it has no branches by construction.

  Funcs are different ‚ÄĒ they have full if/for/match/return. So your question is
  the right one, and the answer is: branches aren't what makes code GPU-unsafe.

  A GPU is SIMT ‚ÄĒ a warp/wavefront runs N lanes in lockstep under an execution 
  mask. A data-dependent branch doesn't crash it; the hardware predicates: lanes
  where the condition is true run the then side with the others masked off,
  then it flips the mask for the else side, then reconverges. The compiler can
  also if-convert a small branch into exactly the select/predicated form before
  it ever reaches the GPU. Either way a pure, bounded branch lowers correctly.
  What you pay is divergence ‚ÄĒ both sides of the branch get executed by the warp
  ‚ÄĒ which is a performance cost, not a correctness one.

  So the actual bars for "can this lower to a kernel at all" are:

  1. No effects ‚ÄĒ no writes to arbitrary memory, no syscalls, no I/O. This is
  exactly the func purity rule. (And it's why an effectful func would break GPU
  lowering ‚ÄĒ not the branches, the effects.)
  2. Statically bounded control flow ‚ÄĒ no unbounded/recursive loops with no
  compile-time trip bound. arche's no-dynamic-allocation ethos already pushes
  everything toward static bounds, so funcs land here naturally.

  Branches clear both bars via predication/if-conversion. Purity + bounds is
  what "GPU-safe" means; branchlessness is not required for correctness.

  Which reframes the map rule: maps forbid branches and force select not because
  branches are unsafe, but to upgrade the guarantee from "correct on GPU" to 
  "divergence-free on GPU." A map promises a clean, predictable,
  vectorizes-and-dispatches-identically kernel ‚ÄĒ so it bans the construct that
  could diverge. A func keeps branches because it trades that performance
  guarantee for ordinary scalar expressiveness.

  So, to state it correctly this time: a func is "GPU-safe" in the sense that
  it's purely lowerable (no effects, bounded) ‚ÄĒ its branches predicate fine.
  It's just not divergence-free the way a map is. That's the line between the
  two.

‚úĽ Churned for 1m 6s

‚ĚĮ so what does the render_all proc look like?                                   
‚óŹ Here's the thing ‚ÄĒ render_all as I wrote it earlier was cheating, and it's the
  perfect place to feel the model bite. It was:

  render_all :: proc()() {
    for (i := 0; i < 10; i = i + 1) {
      render_row(i)();        // proc ‚Üí proc ‚ÄĒ this is exactly what we banned
    }
  }

  So under our own rules, that render_all can't exist. Good ‚ÄĒ let's see what it
  has to become. Two honest answers:

  Answer A ‚ÄĒ inline it (render_row dies)

  A proc is allowed to have a for loop, call externs (sprintf), and perform
  effects. The only thing it can't do is call another arche proc. So you absorb
  render_row's body into the loop:

  render_all :: proc()() {              // inferred: performs {Out}
    for (i := 0; i < 10; i = i + 1) {
      buf: [64]char;
      fmt.sprintf(_, "row %d\n", i)(buf, n:);   // extern = the FFI floor,
  allowed
      emit(buf[0: n]);                          // perform Out, allowed
    }
  }

  This is a legal, flat proc. The cost is the one we flagged in "Bucket 2":
  render_row as a reusable unit is gone.

  Answer B ‚ÄĒ realize render_all was never a leaf (recommended)

  Look at what render_all actually did: nothing but loop-and-invoke. It had no
  work of its own ‚ÄĒ it was orchestration wearing a proc costume. And
  orchestration is exactly what the system layer is for. So you keep render_row
  as a flat leaf and move the iteration up into the glue:

  render_row :: proc(row: int)() {      // flat leaf; inferred: performs {Out}
    buf: [64]char;
    fmt.sprintf(_, "row %d\n", row)(buf, n:);
    emit(buf[0: n]);
  }

  report :: system {
    handle Out with to_file("out.txt");
    for row in 0 .. 10 { render_row(row); }   // iteration is glue ‚ÄĒ the
  proc-analogue of `run`
  }

  render_all simply doesn't exist as a proc. It was a loop, and the loop is now
  system glue ‚ÄĒ the exact same role run plays for a map. If the rows are a pool,
  it reads even more naturally:

  report :: system {
    handle Out with to_file("out.txt");
    for r in Rows { render_row(r.id); }       // fan the leaf out over the pool
  }

  The principle this exposes

  A proc whose entire body is "loop and call another proc" is precisely the proc
  the ban dissolves. Its content is orchestration, and orchestration lives in
  the system. So the layers sort themselves cleanly:

  - render_row ‚ÄĒ a leaf: does real work, performs Out, flat, no proc calls.
  - the loop ‚ÄĒ glue: lives in report, the system that also installs the Out
  handler.

  Notice you never wrote render_all or threaded a sink: the leaf performs emit,
  the system iterates the leaf and handles emit. The two pains from the very
  first version ‚ÄĒ the proc‚Üíproc call and the plumbed fd ‚ÄĒ both evaporated.

  The open seam (same one as before, now sharp)

  This only works if bounded iteration counts as glue. for row in 0..10 and for 
  r in Rows are control flow ‚ÄĒ but they're the same control flow run already
  encodes (iterate a collection, invoke a unit per element). So the line I'd
  draw: iterating a range or a pool to fan out a leaf is glue; arbitrary 
  computation (arithmetic, data-dependent branching, accumulation) is not ‚ÄĒ that
  has to be inside a proc/func/map.

  The case that tests it: what about for r in Rows { if r.dirty { 
  render_row(r.id); } } ‚ÄĒ a guarded fan-out? Is the guard still glue, or is that
  the system starting to grow the logic we said it shouldn't have? That's the
  boundary worth nailing down next ‚ÄĒ it decides whether system stays pure wiring
  or quietly becomes a little language.

‚úĽ Crunched for 1m 20s

‚ĚĮ I think that makes sense. But I will never EVER implement for _ in range.     
  EVER                                                                          

‚óŹ Ha ‚ÄĒ fair, and you're right: I imported a for ... in that arche explicitly
  doesn't have. arche's rule is already "no for x in; collection iteration is
  run + the runtime owns the loop." I broke your own model. Let me fix it
  properly.

  The fan-out primitive already exists: run. A map is fanned over a pool by run.
  So an effectful per-row leaf is fanned the same way ‚ÄĒ run works on a proc
  too. The proc is just the effectful, branchy, sequential sibling of the map:

  // binds a row's columns by query, like a map ‚ÄĒ but it's a PROC, so it may
  // branch and perform effects. The runtime owns the loop.
  render_row :: proc (query { id }) () {
    buf: [64]char;
    fmt.sprintf(_, "row %d\n", id)(buf, n:);
    emit(buf[0: n]);                          // performs {Out}
  }

  report :: system {
    handle Out with to_file("out.txt");
    run render_row;                           // fan the leaf over the pool ‚ÄĒ no
  for-in
  }

  run render_row is to a proc what run step is to a map. The split stays exactly
  the one you already have:

  - run map ‚Üí pure, branch-free, vectorized / GPU.
  - run proc ‚Üí effectful, branchy, sequential, effects resolved by installed
  handlers.

  The runtime owns iteration in both cases. There is no loop in the glue.

  This actually closes the open seam from last turn

  I was worried the system would grow for/if and become a little language. With
  run as the only iteration primitive, it can't ‚ÄĒ and the guard case resolves
  cleanly too. if r.dirty doesn't become system control flow; it becomes one of:

  run render_row over dirty;          // a query filter ‚ÄĒ data matching, not
  control flow

  or a branch inside the leaf:

  render_row :: proc (query { id, dirty }) () {
    if (dirty == 0) { return; }       // per-row branch lives in the proc, where
  branches belong
    ...
    emit(buf[0: n]);
  }

  Either way the system body stays pure wiring: handle + run + sequencing, no 
  for, no if. The branching lives where branching already lives (a proc body or
  a query), and iteration lives where it already lives (the runtime, via run).

  And the genuinely-counted case

  If you ever have "do this N times with no data" ‚ÄĒ that's not collection
  iteration, it's a counted loop, and it goes inside a proc as the C-style for
  arche already has:

  blink :: proc()() {
    for (i := 0; i < 3; i = i + 1) { emit("tick\n"); }   // counted loop: inside
  a proc, fine
  }

  Never at the glue level. So the rule lands as: the system fans out with run 
  and never loops; counted loops live inside procs; collection iteration is 
  always the runtime's job. render_all was the thing that didn't fit, and now
  it's gone in every form ‚ÄĒ replaced by run render_row plus a leaf that owns its
  one row.

‚úĽ Cooked for 1m 3s

‚ĚĮ well that example is dumb because you should iterate over the data you want   
  not check it a runtime. Query for clean data in the first place               

‚óŹ Right ‚ÄĒ that's the actual data-oriented discipline, and it kills my if
  cleanly: you don't iterate-then-test, you query the set so the data you touch 
  is the condition. The guard shouldn't exist. That tightens the thesis instead
  of complicating it: the system never branches, because selection is a property
  of which data, not a runtime check.

  So the principle locks in as: a condition is either structural (which 
  pool/query ‚ÄĒ decided by layout) or produced as data (a column), and if never 
  decides membership or whether-to-process a row. It only survives inside a leaf
  reasoning about its own row's internals.

  The one place that's worth being precise, because it's where "just query it"
  needs a mechanism: dynamic conditions (e.g. health < 10 ‚ÄĒ a continuous value
  you can't pre-partition by every threshold). The honest answer splits on pure
  vs. effectful, and you already do the pure half:

  Pure work (a map): the condition stays in-kernel as a branch-free mask ‚ÄĒ which
  is exactly your dampen example:

  dampen :: map (query { vel, pos }) {
    vel = vel * (pos > 10);              // the condition is a value (0/1),
  applied as data
    vel = select(pos > 100.0, 0.0, vel);
  }

  No membership test, no divergence ‚ÄĒ the predicate is just arithmetic over the
  whole column.

  Effectful work (a run proc): you can't select away a side effect ‚ÄĒ you can't
  "not emit" by multiplying by zero. So the matching rows have to be a real set
  before the run. The data-oriented move is to compact them into a contiguous 
  range first ‚ÄĒ the permute/partition + live-count machinery you already have
  for sort ‚ÄĒ and then run over that range:

  tick :: system {
    run partition_alive;     // reorder so the rows you want are contiguous
  (data reorg)
    run emit_alive;          // run the effectful leaf over exactly that set ‚ÄĒ
  no per-row if
  }

  The selection became a data-reorganization step, not a branch in the consumer.
  Same principle, just made physical because the consumer has effects.

  So the model holds all the way down: the system is pure wiring (handle + run),
  selection lives in queries and in data you produce (masks, partitions), and
  the only if left in the whole language is a leaf reasoning about its own row ‚ÄĒ
  never about which rows exist. The if r.dirty version was the imperative
  reflex; "query clean data" is the version that actually fits what you're
  building.

‚úĽ Churned for 1m 13s

‚ÄĽ recap: We're designing an arche language variant that bans proc‚Üíproc calls, using algebraic effects plus a system/schedule layer for composition. Next: nail down handler precedence and setup/teardown ordering when a system installs multiple handlers.

‚ĚĮ I would completely remove if if I could.                                      
                                                                                
  anyways. Back to the examples                                                 
                                                                                
  im wondering if the query signature should change. Because its not a runtime  
  param.                                                                        
                                                                                
  maybe something like                                                          
                                                                                
  foo :: map query {bar}                                                        
                                                                                
  foo :: proc query{bar}()()                                                    

‚óŹ Your instinct is right, and there's a stronger reason for it than aesthetics:
  the query isn't just "not a runtime param" ‚ÄĒ it's a different axis of input.
  The query is the per-row, varying data (the iteration space, bound from
  columns, compile-time-resolved). A runtime param is uniform ‚ÄĒ one value,
  constant across the whole fan-out. That's exactly the GPU distinction between
  a per-thread attribute and a uniform, so separating them in the syntax is
  semantically correct, not just tidy.

  So I'd lift the query out, right after the kind keyword, in query {} form so
  it never reads like (args):

  name :: <kind> query { per-row columns } ( uniforms ) ( out )

  - query { ‚Ä¶ } ‚ÄĒ per-row, from the pool, compile-time. The varying axis.
  - ( ‚Ä¶ ) ‚ÄĒ uniform runtime in-params, bound once at the run site.
  - ( ‚Ä¶ ) ‚ÄĒ out-params.

  And empty lists drop off. That gives you a clean, unified picture across all
  three kinds:

  // map: pure, branch-free, vectorized. per-row cols + optional uniform.
  integrate :: map  query { pos, vel } (dt: float)   { pos = pos + vel * dt; }
  dampen    :: map  query { vel, pos }               { vel = vel * (pos > 10); }

  // proc fanned over the pool: effectful/branchy/sequential. query present =
  "run over data".
  render_row :: proc query { id }                    { fmt.sprintf(...)(buf,
  n:); emit(buf[0:n]); }

  // proc one-shot: NO query = "invoked once". classic (in)(out).
  load_tx    :: proc (path: []char) (count: int)     { ... }

  // func: pure value, never fanned, no query. unchanged.
  area       :: func (w: int, h: int) -> int         { return w * h; }

  Two things fall out of this that I think are wins:

  1. Presence of query encodes the execution mode. A signature with query {} is
  fanned by run over a pool; one without is a one-shot. You can tell how a proc
  runs from its head, before reading the body. map always has a query (it's
  nothing but a fanned kernel); func never does.
  2. Uniforms get bound at the run site, per-row data binds automatically. That
  answers "where does dt come from" cleanly:

  tick :: system {
    run integrate(dt);     // dt uniform passed once here; pos/vel bound per-row
  from the pool
    run render_row;        // no uniforms; id bound per-row
  }

  The one thing this forces you to decide: does a fanned unit have an out-list 
  at all? A map writes its result into the queried columns (pos = ‚Ä¶), not into
  an out-param. A fanned proc does the same plus performs effects. So for fanned
  units, the ( out ) slot may be dead ‚ÄĒ the "output" is the column write and
  the effects. Out-params might only exist on one-shot procs (load_tx ‚Ä¶ 
  (count:)). If that holds, the shape simplifies further: query + (uniforms) for
  fanned things, (in)(out) for one-shot things, and the two never mix. Worth
  checking against a real case before committing ‚ÄĒ is there a fanned proc that
  genuinely needs a scalar out-param rather than writing a column?

  (And your "remove if entirely" aside ‚ÄĒ you're closer than you'd think. Between
  query-selection picking the set and select handling per-row value choices,
  the only if left is a leaf's own early-return logic, and most of those
  collapse to select or a tighter query too. Probably not zero, but the language
  can make if the rare exception rather than the backbone.)

‚úĽ Baked for 1m 29s

‚ĚĮ fundementally it doesn't make sense because (in) is the same for the loop,    
  but (out) would only work for the last value. That is strange. Im thinking    
  that these are different decl entirely                                        

‚óŹ Yes ‚ÄĒ you've found the actual fault line. Out-params are inherently a 
  call-once concept: a caller allocates a slot, the callee writes it once. Fan
  that over N rows and the single slot is meaningless ‚ÄĒ last-write-wins, which
  is nonsense. Your (in) is fine because uniforms genuinely are constant across 
  the loop; it's (out) that has no coherent meaning over a fan-out. That
  asymmetry is the language telling you these aren't one decl with an optional
  query ‚ÄĒ they're two different shapes.

  So the honest carve is by I/O shape, and there are two worlds:

  Call-once ‚ÄĒ caller passes args, gets slots back:
  divmod :: proc (a: int, b: int)(q: int, r: int)   // args in, slots out, runs
  exactly once
  load_tx :: proc (path: []char)(count: int)
  area :: func (w: int, h: int) -> int

  Fan-out ‚ÄĒ runtime owns the loop, binds per-row; no out-params at all:
  integrate  :: map  query { pos, vel } (dt: float)   // per-row cols + uniform;
  output = column writes
  render_row :: <fanned-proc> query { id }            // per-row; output =
  column writes + effects

  A fan-out's "output" is the per-row column write (in place) or effects ‚ÄĒ never
  a scalar slot. And when you genuinely need one scalar out of many rows,
  that's not an out-param either, it's the operation you already have in the IR:
  a reduce (or scan). That's the only legitimate scalar result of a loop:

  total :: reduce query { revenue } -> float (+)      // fold N rows ‚Üí 1 value,
  with a monoid

  So the single-out-slot weirdness isn't a syntax wart to paper over ‚ÄĒ it's
  pointing at a real kind boundary: scalar-out-of-many is a fold, and it 
  deserves its own decl (which maps straight onto your PAR_REDUCE). Out-params
  stay in the call-once world; fan-out outputs are columns, effects, or a
  reduction.

  That leaves exactly one fork for you to call:

  Is the effectful fan-out a distinct kind from map, or is "vectorizable" just 
  an inferred property of a single fanned kind? Two coherent answers:

  - Distinct kinds. map keeps its hard contract (branch-free, pure,
  vectorizes/GPU ‚ÄĒ enforced by E0046), and the effectful sibling is its own
  keyword. You read the kind and you know the guarantee. Costs you a fourth
  keyword (and it needs a name ‚ÄĒ step? each? pass? since proc is now the
  call-once one and system is the composer).
  - One fanned kind, derived guarantee. You write one fanned declaration; the
  compiler infers (effects + branch-freedom) whether it vectorizes, and @gpu
  asserts it must. Fewer keywords, but map's "guaranteed GPU-safe" contract
  becomes a checked property rather than a visible kind.

  Given how much you value the kind telling you the execution mode (we just
  established query-presence signals fan-out), I'd lean distinct kinds ‚ÄĒ keep
  map as the word that means "this provably vectorizes," and give the effectful
  fan-out its own name. But that's the call: do you want the GPU guarantee to be
  a keyword or a derived-and-asserted property?

‚úĽ Baked for 3m 13s

‚ĚĮ what do you mean by per row buffer columnbs                                   

‚óŹ Concretely: in the staged server pipeline, each connection is a row in the
  Conn pool, and the stages hand data down by reading/writing that row's
  columns:

  recv_step   writes  inbuf, nread        ‚Üź bytes off the socket
  parse_step  reads   inbuf  ‚Üí writes method/target columns
  serve_step  writes  status, outbuf      ‚Üź the response bytes
  send_step   reads   outbuf, status      ‚Üí bytes to the socket

  So the receive buffer and send buffer for a connection have to live somewhere
  that persists across stages ‚ÄĒ recv fills it, send (a different system, a
  different tick maybe) drains it. In a normal call-stack server those are just
  stack locals inside the request handler (buf: [1024]char on the frame). But
  there's no persistent per-request frame here ‚ÄĒ the connection outlives any
  single proc invocation. So the buffer has to become part of the row, i.e. a
  column:

  Conn :: arche {
    fd :: int, nread :: int, status :: int,    // scalar columns ‚ÄĒ trivial, this
  is what SoA does
    inbuf :: [1024]char,                        // ‚Üź a buffer COLUMN: each row
  owns 1024 bytes
    outbuf :: [1024]char,
  }
  [256]Conn(0);

  That inbuf :: [1024]char is the thing I called a "per-row buffer column," and
  it's awkward for three reasons specific to arche:

  1. Does the column model even allow an array element type? A scalar column
  pos_x :: float lowers to [N]float ‚ÄĒ one value per row, the SoA sweet spot. A
  buffer column would be [256][1024]char ‚ÄĒ a column whose element is itself an 
  array. Whether arche permits that as a column type is the open question. If it
  doesn't, you literally can't hold the transient bytes in the pool, and ¬ß7.4
  falls apart.
  2. Layout fights SoA. Scalar columns want to be contiguous-per-field (all the
  nreads together) so a map can stride them. A byte buffer wants each
  connection's 1024 bytes contiguous per row (you recv into it and pass it as
  one slice). So a buffer column has the opposite layout from every scalar
  column in the same archetype ‚ÄĒ you'd have a mixed AoS-within-SoA archetype.
  3. The no-heap tax is brutal here. 256 conns √ó 1024 bytes √ó 2 buffers = 512 KB
  reserved always, because every connection slot must statically reserve a full
  max-size buffer whether it's a 12-byte request or a 1 KB one. Stack locals
  only cost while a request is live; columns cost the worst case, permanently.

  So the real design question is how a pool row carries a chunk of variable-ish 
  scratch bytes. Two ways out if buffer-columns are too ugly:

  - Slab + offset columns. One big static byte arena; Conn keeps only scalar
  columns inbuf_off :: int, inbuf_len :: int pointing into it. Rows stay pure
  SoA; the bytes live in a shared pool. This is the standard data-oriented
  answer for variable-length/blob data ‚ÄĒ but it hands you a sub-allocation
  problem inside the arena (bump + free on connection close, or fixed per-row
  slots, or a ring).
  - Sidecar arena indexed by row. A plain static inbufs : [256][1024]char
  global, where conn row i uses inbufs[i] ‚ÄĒ the same bytes, just not modeled as
  a column. Simplest, but it's "a column by hand" living outside the archetype,
  so queries/run can't see it as part of the row.

  That's why I flagged it as the likely real blocker: the whole "deep call stack
  ‚Üí wide data pipeline" reframe assumes per-request transient state can move
  from the stack into the pool, and scalars move trivially but byte buffers 
  don't ‚ÄĒ they force a decision about how columnar, statically-allocated rows
  hold blob data.

‚úĽ Saut√©ed for 46s

‚ĚĮ an archetype is for types, not buffers. So this raises another question       
                                                                                
  [N]foo (buffer) and [N]foo (type) are identical                               
  ‚éŅ ¬†Interrupted ¬∑ What should Claude do instead?

‚ĚĮ actually its not, because if its on the RHS then it must be a type, no?       

‚óŹ Right ‚ÄĒ you've got it, and the self-correction is the key: they're 
  disambiguated by position, so they're not identical.

  - In a type position ‚ÄĒ the RHS of ::, after a :, after ->, or as a
  column/field type ‚ÄĒ [N]foo can only mean the type "array of N foo." There's
  nothing to allocate there; you're describing a shape.
  - As a statement with a live-count ‚ÄĒ [256]Conn(0), [8]Player(5) ‚ÄĒ [N]foo is a
  pool: storage for N entities.

  The (count) is the tell. A pool gets a live-count; a type never does. So
  [1024]char (no count, in a field) and [256]Conn(0) (count, at statement level)
  are never confusable.

  But it's worth seeing why the position rule is sound ‚ÄĒ it's not a parsing
  trick, it's a kind distinction:

  - A pool is a queryable collection of entities ‚ÄĒ it has identity, supports
  insert/delete/query, and is the thing run/map fan over. It's storage.
  - An array type is a flat value. No identity, no query, no
  iteration-as-entities. It's a shape.

  "An archetype is for types, not buffers" is exactly this: the archetype
  declares column types; the pool is the buffer of those types.

  And that fully dissolves the worry I raised. The clean rule falls out:

  ‚Ėé A column's type may be an array [K]T (a flat per-row buffer) ‚ÄĒ it's in type 
  ‚Ėé position, so it's unambiguous. A column may not be a pool ‚ÄĒ a pool isn't a 
  ‚Ėé type; you can't nest an entity-collection inside a row.

  So inbuf :: [1024]char is just a normal array-typed column ‚ÄĒ well-formed, no
  ambiguity. The thing that's genuinely illegal is inbuf :: [1024]Conn(0) ‚ÄĒ
  trying to put a pool in a row. I was conflating "buffer" (pool, a
  storage/entity concept) with "array-typed value" (a type). In type position
  there's no conflation, because a pool literally cannot appear there.

  Which means the per-row buffer isn't a semantic blocker at all ‚ÄĒ it's a normal
  array column. What's left is only:

  1. Memory ‚ÄĒ 256 √ó 1024 reserved statically, worst-case, always (the no-heap
  tax, real but not ambiguous).
  2. Layout ‚ÄĒ does the codegen stride that array column SoA-style or keep each
  row's 1024 bytes contiguous? (You want contiguous, so you can hand it to
  recv/send as one slice.)

  Both are codegen decisions, not language-design ambiguities. So the server
  pipeline's transient state can live in the pool after all ‚ÄĒ buffer columns are
  just array-typed columns.

  Want me to fix ¬ß7.4 / open-question #3 in the doc to reflect this ‚ÄĒ reframe it
  from "is this even expressible?" to "it's an array column; the open part is
  layout + the static-memory cost"?
