‚óŹ Now the two open questions that this resolves/changes:

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)
  ‚éŅ ¬†Interrupted ¬∑ What should Claude do instead?

‚ĚĮ that is impossible. also please don't edit the doc without asking me if what  
  you said even makes sense                                                     
‚óŹ You're right on both counts ‚ÄĒ let me stop and fix the thinking before touching
  the doc.

  What's impossible: "a pure func that writes into a borrowed buffer." Writing
  memory the caller can see is a side effect ‚ÄĒ so that function isn't pure, by
  definition. I even hedged it ("this requires funcs to write a borrowed
  buffer") which should've been the red flag that the whole idea was incoherent.

  And that error infects the extern func edit I just made: I listed sprintf and 
  memcpy as "pure utilities," but they're not ‚ÄĒ they write their output buffer,
  which is an effect. So they can't be extern func (pure). I grouped them
  wrongly with the genuinely-pure ones.

  Here's the corrected picture, which I think is actually fine:

  - Genuinely pure foreign funcs = read inputs, return a value, write nothing:
  sqrt, sin, strlen, atoi, hash(bytes) -> u64. These are real and common, so
  extern func does substantially fix the "impractical" complaint ‚ÄĒ math,
  parsing-to-a-scalar, hashing, length all become cheap leaf calls.
  - Buffer-writers (sprintf, memcpy) are effectful ‚ÄĒ they mutate memory. They
  are not extern func; they're effects (or something else). My edit is wrong
  about them.

  Which leaves the real question I should have asked instead of inventing an
  impossible answer: how does a leaf produce bytes at all? Given no heap + funcs
  return one value + writing borrowed memory = effect, the options are:

  1. Return by value ‚ÄĒ fmt_row(...) -> [64]char (or a {buf, len} struct), copied
  to the caller's slot. That's genuinely pure (a return, not a write),
  heap-free, just a stack-sized copy. Works only if arche funcs can return
  fixed-size aggregates by value.
  2. It's an effect ‚ÄĒ byte production performs a Buf/Write effect. Honest, but
  heavy for "format a number."
  3. Writing a local buffer is pure (internal mutation isn't an external effect)
  ‚ÄĒ but then you can't get the bytes out of the func without either returning
  by value (#1) or an effect (#2).

  I lean #1 ‚ÄĒ a pure formatter returns its bytes by value rather than writing a
  borrowed buffer ‚ÄĒ but it hinges on whether arche allows aggregate
  return-by-value, and it's your call.

  So before I edit ¬ß2, #8, or #10: does that hold up to you ‚ÄĒ extern func only
  for value-returning pure foreign funcs (sqrt/strlen/hash), and byte-production
  handled by return-by-value (#1)? Or do you see byte-production differently? I
  won't touch the doc until you say the model's right.

‚úĽ Baked for 1m 39s

‚ĚĮ foreign func will never be a thing                                            
                                                                                
  but many of these things can be functions if a function could return an       
  effect if that makes sense (still couldn't be foreign)                        
                                                                                
  also maybe this means many stdlib stuff should be implemented as handlers?    

‚óŹ Got it ‚ÄĒ drop extern func entirely (foreign stays always-effectful extern 
  proc). And yes, "a function that returns an effect" makes sense ‚ÄĒ it's a real
  and well-founded idea. Let me check my understanding of it before I touch
  anything.

  "A func returns an effect" = effects become first-class values. A func builds
  and returns an unperformed effect (a description), which stays pure because
  returning an effect ‚Č† performing it. Something else ‚ÄĒ a proc, or a handler ‚ÄĒ
  performs it later. This is exactly the IO-value / free-monad shape: pure code
  constructs the effect, the impure edge runs it.

  And it cleanly dissolves the byte-production problem that tripped us up:

  - fmt_int :: func(x: int) -> Emit returns a description ‚ÄĒ Emit{ fmt: "%d", 
  args: [x] } ‚ÄĒ a small, fixed-size value. Pure: it never touches bytes.
  - The handler is the only place bytes get rendered and written (it can ‚ÄĒ it's
  effectful).

  So pure code produces plans, handlers produce bytes. No
  buffer-writing-in-a-func, no extern func, no allocation. The func is pure
  because it returns data; the effect is the handler's.

  For this to stay arche-shaped, two constraints:
  1. Effect-values must be bounded (op tag + fixed args, no heap) ‚ÄĒ fine for a
  fmt-spec + a small arg list.
  2. Keep it first-order ‚ÄĒ a func returns an effect; a proc performs it. Don't
  let effect-values chain monadically (bind), or you've imported the heavy
  machinery we wanted to avoid.

  (One nice bonus: since constructing an effect-value is pure, a map could fill
  a column with effect-descriptions per row ‚ÄĒ GPU-pure ‚ÄĒ and a later effectful
  pass drains them. Command-buffer generation, basically. Advanced, not core,
  but it falls out for free.)

  Stdlib as handlers ‚ÄĒ yes, that's the direct consequence. If fmt/io/net ops are
  effects, then the stdlib is largely effect declarations + handlers (mostly
  runtime-provided): the pure surface is funcs that build effect-values + the
  data types, and the handlers do the rendering/syscalls. fmt.sprintf stops
  being a #foreign proc you call and becomes "a Fmt effect, rendered by a
  handler." That's a cleaner stdlib shape, and it's what ¬ß9 #8 was already
  gesturing at.

  So, before I edit ‚ÄĒ here's what I'd change, and I want your read first:
  1. Revert the extern func paragraph in ¬ß2 (it's wrong; foreign = extern 
  proc/effect only).
  2. Add to ¬ß5: effects are first-class values; a func may return one (pure); a 
  proc performs it ‚ÄĒ with the bounded + first-order caveats.
  3. State the principle: much of the stdlib is effect-declarations + handlers;
  pure helpers return effect-values.
  4. Resolve #10: formatting is a func returning a Fmt effect-value, rendered by
  a handler ‚ÄĒ not a pure buffer-writer, not foreign.

  Does that match what you mean by "a function could return an effect" ‚ÄĒ and is
  the bounded / first-order constraint acceptable, or were you picturing
  something looser? Once you confirm, I'll make exactly those edits.

      255 -  setup       { io.fopen_write(path)(fd:); }         // resour
          -ce lifecycle owned by the handler                             
      256 -  emit(line)  { io.fwrite(fd, line, line.length); resume; }   
      257 -  teardown    { io.fclose(move fd); }

      377  barrier. ¬ß8.4's `insert Conn rows` / `delete done rows` depend

      379  
      380 +### Effects as values (a func may return one)                 
      381 +                                                              
      382 +An effect need not be performed where it's named. **Effects ar
          +e first-class values:** you                                   
      383 +can construct an *unperformed* effect ‚ÄĒ a description ‚ÄĒ and re
          +turn it. This stays pure,                                     
      384 +because **returning an effect ‚Č† performing it**:              
      385 +                                                              
      386 +```arche                                                      
      387 +fmt_int :: func(x: int) -> Emit { return Emit("%d", x); }   //
          + pure: builds a description, performs nothing                 
      388 +                                                              
      389 +show :: each query { id } {                                   
      390 +  perform fmt_int(id);     // a proc/each performs the effect 
          +the func returned                                             
      391 +}                                                             
      392 +```                                                           
      393 +                                                              
      394 +Pure code (including `func`s and `map`s) builds the *plan*; a 
          +`proc`/`each` (or the                                         
      395 +runtime) *performs* it; the *handler* does the actual work ‚ÄĒ e
          +.g. rendering bytes and                                       
      396 +writing them. This is the clean answer to "how does pure code 
          +do effectful-looking                                          
      397 +work": it **returns** the effect, it doesn't perform it. (It's
          + the IO-value / free-monad                                    
      398 +shape ‚ÄĒ see below.)                                           
      399 +                                                              
      400 +Two constraints keep it arche-shaped:                         
      401 +- **Bounded.** An effect-value is a fixed-size description (op
          + tag + bounded args) ‚ÄĒ no                                     
      402 +  heap. A format effect carries a spec + a small arg list, *no
          +t* rendered bytes.                                            
      403 +- **First-order.** A func returns *an* effect; a proc performs
          + it. Effect-values don't                                      
      404 +  chain monadically (`bind`) ‚ÄĒ that would drag in the continua
          +tion/heap machinery the                                       
      405 +  design avoids.                                              
      406 +                                                              
      407 +And it's why **byte production is never a pure buffer-write** 
          +(writing memory is an                                         
      408 +effect): a pure formatter *returns* a description, and the **h
          +andler** is the only place                                    
      409 +bytes are rendered and written.                               
      410 +                                                              
      411  ### Relationship to monads
      412  
      413  Effects and monads solve the same problem (structured effects 
           in a pure setting).

      277 +use intrinsics incl. the handler-only `os.*`, write state, fai
          +l via a policy) plus what its position grants:                
      278  **`resume`** (hand control back to the performer) and an optio
           nal `setup`/`teardown`
      279  lifecycle bracketing its install extent. It is *not* "a proc t
           hat can call procs" ‚ÄĒ the
      280  proc‚Üíproc ban applies to it too: a handler cannot call an arch
           e proc, cannot `run`,

      332

          -have **ambient handlers                                       
      335 -provided by the runtime** (installed at the root), so a leaf m
          -ay perform them with no                                       
      336 -`handle` in scope. (That is what "the io/net/fs stdlib is effe
          -ct-declarations + runtime                                     
      337 -handlers" means ‚ÄĒ ¬ß8.2.) *User-defined* effects (e.g. an `Out`
          - that writes formatted                                        
      334 +`Net`, `Time`, randomness, `Fmt` (byte rendering), and structu
          +ral `insert`/`delete` ‚ÄĒ                                       
      335 +have **ambient handlers provided by the runtime** (installed a
          +t the root), so a leaf may                                    
      336 +perform them with no `handle` in scope. More broadly, **much o
          +f the stdlib (`fmt`, `io`,                                    
      337 +`net`) is effect declarations + handlers**: the pure surface i
          +s `func`s that build                                          
      338 +effect-values + data types, and the handlers (mostly runtime-p
          +rovided) do the rendering                                     
      339 +and syscalls (¬ß8.2). *User-defined* effects (e.g. an `Out` tha
          +t writes formatted                                            
      340  lines to one chosen file) still need a `system` to install a h
           andler.
      341

           .open` / `os.close` /

‚óŹ Verified clean. The two remaining extern func hits are both the negative
  mentions ("there is no extern func") in open questions #8 and #10 ‚ÄĒ correct.
  No buffer-writing funcs left (fmt_row/fmt_id/into:/"borrowed buffer" all
  gone), and sprintf survives only in the ¬ß8.1 "Today" block (the code being
  migrated from, which is right).

  So the doc now matches your model exactly:

  - No extern func. Foreign code is always extern proc ‚Üí an effect, behind a
  handler (¬ß2).
  - Pure floor = native funcs + a closed set of intrinsics (select, arithmetic,
  slicing) ‚ÄĒ not formatting, not foreign.
  - Funcs can return effect-values ‚ÄĒ a func builds an unperformed effect (a
  description) and returns it, staying pure; a proc performs it later (new ¬ß5
  "Effects as values," with the bounded + first-order constraints).
  - Byte production is an effect. Pure code builds Fmt/Emit descriptions; the
  handler is the only place bytes get rendered and written. #10 resolved on
  those terms.
  - Much of the stdlib (fmt/io/net) = effect declarations + handlers (¬ß5
  "Runtime effects").
  - Examples fixed: Out.emit carries a spec + args; leaves emit("%d", x)
  (perform, no rendered bytes); handlers render-then-os.write. The
  handler-powers line no longer says "call externs" (it's os.* intrinsics now).

  That resolves the impracticality you flagged without the impossible
  pure-buffer-writer and without foreign funcs: pure code stays pure by
  returning plans, and the work lands in handlers.
